## 2.4.3. Discussion on clinical efficacy

The MAH submitted ASCENT-03 study results to support an extension of indication for Trodelvy as monotherapy for the treatment of adult patients with unresectable locally advanced or metastatic TNBC who have not received prior systemic therapy for metastatic disease and who are not candidates for PD-1 or PD-L1 inhibitor therapy.

## Design and conduct of clinical studies

The global, open-label study ASCENT-03 enrolled 558 patients with locally advanced, inoperable, or metastatic TNBC who have not received previous systemic therapy for advanced disease. Patients could have received (neo)adjuvant chemotherapy (including anti-PD-(L)1 treatment) if this was completed ≥ 6 months before disease recurrence. Previous treatment with a topoisomerase 1 inhibitors or antibody drug conjugates containing a topoisomerase inhibitor was not allowed. Patients should not have been eligible for PD-(L)1 inhibitor therapy.

Patients were randomized (1:1) to receive either sacituzumab govitecan (SG) or treatment of physician's choice chemotherapy (TPC). SG was applied in the approved dosing regimen of 10 mg/kg IV on Days 1 and 8 of 21-day cycles. TPC was selected prior to randomization to be either paclitaxel, nab-paclitaxel or gemcitabine/carboplatin by the investigator. Retreatment with taxane, gemcitabine, or platinum agents were allowed in case of a treatmentfree interval of ≥ 12 months. Patients should have received prior anthracycline in the (neo)adjuvant setting or be considered not eligible for anthracyclines as assessed by the treating physician. The choice of chemotherapies and their dosing

\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

regimens is in line with guideline recommendations and corresponds to the control arm of studies in similar settings, e.g. KEYNOTE-355.

Participants in the control group were eligible to receive SG in the crossover phase upon disease progression verified by BICR.

The eligibility criteria are overall considered adequate. TNBC status and tumour PD-L1 expression was confirmed centrally on a recent or archival tumour specimen. Tumour PD-L1 CPS was determined using the PD-L1 IHC 22C3 assay.

Patients were stratified by geographic region (US/Canada/Western Europe versus Rest of World), and de novo disease versus recurrent disease with a treatment-free interval of 6-12 months versus recurrent disease &gt; 12 months from completion of treatment in the curative setting. Stratification factors are considered acceptable.

Baseline demographic and disease characteristics were overall balanced between treatment arms and representative of a 1L TNBC study population. Median age was 55 years  with 26% of patients 65 years or over. Most participants were White (64%) or Asian (23%) with only 3% being Black and most participants had a good performance status (ECOG PS 0 66%, PS 1 34%).

The majority of study participants had metastatic disease at screening, the most common sites of metastasis were the lymph nodes (64%), lung (60%), bone (33%), and liver (27%). Only 29 participants (5.2%) were enrolled with stable brain metastasis. BRCA 1/2 mutation status was known for 76% of participants, out of which 18% of tested study participants had positive BRCA mutations. More than half of the study participants (67.4%) received prior (neo)adjuvant systemic treatments, most commonly taxanes (58.1%), anthracyclines (55%), capecitabine (19.2%) and platinum (17.9%). 4.3% of patients received prior anti-PD-(L)1 agents. Most patients also had prior radiotherapy (58.2%).

Nearly all study participants had metastatic TNBC and only 12 patients (2.2%) were enrolled with locally advanced and inoperable disease. No subgroup results by disease status were provided, but the sample size of patients with unresectable locally advanced disease is considered too small for reliable conclusions. Disease status is unlikely to be an effect modifier and treatment recommendations do not differ between patients with unresectable locally advanced and metastatic disease; therefore, the extrapolation of study results to the small subgroup of patients with unresectable disease is agreed.

The MAH sought an approval for 1L TNBC patients who are 'not candidates for PD-(L)1 inhibitor therapy'. Under the initial versions of the study protocol, recruitment was open for patients with PD-L1 negative (i.e. CPS &lt;10) tumours, and patients whose tumours were PD-L1 positive (i.e. CPS ≥ 10) if they have received an anti-PD-(L)1 inhibitor in the neoadjuvant or adjuvant setting. With the implementation of protocol amendment 2 (dated 30 August 2023, when already 310 participants were randomised), patients with PD-L1 positive tumours were also allowed to be included if they could not receive treatment with an anti-PD-(L)1 agent due to a comorbidity.

However, only two patients with PD-L1 positive tumours were finally enrolled, the clinical study population primarily represents a target population with PD-L1 negative tumours.

There are currently no reliable data available to inform on the use of immune checkpoint inhibitors (ICI) for patients with PD-L1 positive tumours who received an ICI in the curative setting. However, the most recent ESMO guidance recommends a rechallenge with ICI in combination with chemotherapy as 1L treatment for patients with PD-L1 positive tumours and recurrence &gt;6 (-12) months after the end of (neo)adjuvant ICI (ESMO Living guideline updates 2025 10 ). Therefore, only patients with a

10  ESMO Living Guideline for Triple-negative metastatic breast cancer; G Curigliano et al., Ann Oncol. 2025

\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

treatmentfree interval of ≤ 6 months after treatment with curative intent would be clearly no t worse prognosis were not included in study ASCENT-03.

candidates for PD-(L)1 inhibitor therapy. These patients with a more aggressive tumour biology and The MAH argued that patients with PD-L1 positive disease who have a comorbidity precluding treatment with ICI or cannot be rechallenged with ICI upon disease recurrence represent a clinically relevant subgroup with an unmet need. Given that these patients are typically treated with chemotherapy, the results from study ASCENT-03 would be applicable since SG demonstrated superior efficacy compared to chemotherapy. It is acknowledged that the treatment effect of SG is not considered to be associated with tumour PD-L1 expression, i.e. PD-L1 expression is unlikely to be an effect modifier. SG has previously demonstrated efficacy in TNBC patients after prior treatment. In view of the unmet medical need, it might be acceptable to extrapolate the study results to patients with PD-L1 positive tumours who are not eligible for ICI, although these patients were not represented in the study population. Despite the remaining uncertainty on the exact effect size, a broader indication including all patients ineligible for PD-(L)1 inhibitor therapy provides an additional option for individual treatment decisions. However, the study population characteristics are clearly reflected in section 5.1 of the SmPC (. A cross-reference to section 5.1 of the SmPC has been already proposed by the MAH in the indication wording in section 4.1 of the SmPC which is agreed on. For patients with PD-L1 negative mTNBC and germline BRCA mutations, a PARP inhibitor is the preferred 1L treatment option according to clinical guidelines 13 . BRCA1/2 mutation status at baseline was known only for 34.5% of participants. The MAH did not implement the EMA SA recommendations to require gBRCA1/2 testing for all participants and prior PARPi treatment for eligible patients with BRCA1/2 mutant tumours, but referred to global disparities in resources for testing and drug availability. 5.2% of study participants had positive mutations status (n=17 and 12 in the SG and TPC arm, respectively; DCO: 02 April 2025); only 1 participant received prior treatment with a PARPi. The proportion of patients with positive BRCA mutations appeared rather low compared to literature data for TNBC (e.g. 9.3% to 15.4% according to Armstrong 11 ). The primary endpoint for study ASCENT-03 was PFS by BICR per RECIST v1.1; key secondary endpoints were OS, ORR by BICR and quality of life as measured by change from baseline in the physical functioning score and by time to deterioration (TTD) of fatigue from baseline of EORTC QLQC30 V3.0. Duration of response (DOR) and time to response (TTR) by BICR were additional secondary endpoints. The primary endpoint analysis of PFS by BICR served as the gatekeeper for the secondary endpoints:

OS, ORR and analyses for QoL, which were planned to be tested sequentially when the above hypotheses in the hierarchy were statistically significant.

The study planned for 3 interim analyses and 1 final analysis. The first analysis was the final analysis for PFS when 352 PFS events were reached including a descriptive analysis of OS (53% information) at the same time. The remaining 2 interim analyses at 70% and 85% of information and final analysis when 391 deaths would be observed were planned to test OS. To account for the interim analyses of OS, Lan-DeMets alpha spending function approximating an O'Brien Fleming approach was used to adjust the type I error.

Several protocol amendments were implemented in this open-label study that affected changes in the primary efficacy analysis. The rationale for the changes (mainly with the aim to ensure a sufficient follow-up time and data maturity) can be followed based on the provided explanations. A study-specific Data Access Restriction Plan implemented measures to ensure data integrity and regulated that key

11  Armstrong N et al. A systematic review of the international prevalence of BRCA mutation in breast cancer. Clin Epidemiol 2019

\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

personnel (clinical development and study biostatistician) involved with the interpretation of the data, remained blinded to treatment assignment until the database was locked for the primary analysis.

Post-hoc analysis according to the defined analysis of Protocol Amendment 1 and 2 showed consistent results with the primary analysis and indicated that the trial would have met the primary PFS endpoint also with the prior protocol versions.

A high number of important protocol deviation was reported (45.9% across both treatment arms); but after further review it can be agreed that these are not expected to have had a significant impact on the interpretation of the study results overall.

## Efficacy data and additional analyses

Efficacy data were provided from the primary (final) analysis of PFS with a data cu-toff date of 02 April 2025 and a median duration of follow-up of approximately 13 months. Analyses of secondary endpoints of OS, ORR, DOR and TTR by BICR as well as PRO data are descriptive only; key secondary endpoints were not formally tested at the time of the PFS primary analysis based on the testing hierarchy. In addition, updated efficacy data at the time of OS Interim Analysis 1 with a median duration of OS follow-up of 17.7 months (DCO: 27 October 2025) were provided during the procedure.

Study ASCENT-03 demonstrated a statistically significant PFS improvement with SG versus TPC (HR: 0.623; 95% CI: 0.501, 0.775; 2-sided P &lt; 0.0001) at the time of the primary PFS analysis. Median PFS was 9.69 months (95% CI: 8.15, 11.14) in the SG group and 6.93 months (95% CI: 5.62, 8.25) in the TPC group. Results from PFS sensitivity analyses were consistent with the primary analysis.

Updated descriptive PFS data by BICR at the time of OS IA1 showed similar results (PFS HR: 0.65 [95% CI: 0.53, 0.79]).

OS data at the time of the primary PFS analysis were immature with 206 deaths (37% maturity rate; 53% IF) and showed similar results in both treatment arms (HR: 0.985; 95% CI: 0.748, 1.297). Updated OS data at OS IA1 based on 283 events (50.7% maturity) remained overall consistent with longer follow-up. Median OS was 21.45 months (95% CI: 18.86, 26.55) in the SG group and 21.26 months (95% CI: 18.69, 25.17) in the TPC group, the OS HR was 0.953 (95% CI: 0.755, 1.205, P = 0.6860). The OS KM curves largely overlapped until month 24 when they began to separate in favour of SG. Of note, numbers at risk were limited beyond 24 months.

Results do not show an OS advantage of SG over first-line chemotherapy but they can be considered sufficiently mature to exclude an OS detriment. Interpretation of OS data is hampered by the high cross-over rate; at OS IA1, 154 out of 279 (55.2%) participants in the TPC group received SG in the second line setting, 125 participants (44.8%) received SG through the crossover phase of the study. On the contrary, more patients received chemotherapy in the SG arm compared to the TPC arm at disease progression.

It is acknowledged that the ability to demonstrate an OS improvement is impacted by the study design allowing cross-over. Given the established OS benefit of SG compared with TPC in previously treated metastatic TNBC (based on the ASCENT study), sensitivity analyses to adjust for the effect of crossover on OS were prespecified. Results showed HR point estimates (range: 0.622-0.908) in favour of the SG group. Similarly, exploratory analyses to adjust for the treatment switch to second line SG in the TPC group (including also participants who received SG through commercial means) showed HR point estimates between 0.561 and 0.901. The point estimates vary depending on each method and are accompanied by wide confidence intervals which limits the robustness and interpretation of the results. Furthermore, no adjustment was performed in the SG group for 2L treatment, which may have affected the OS and thus adjusted HR. Given the limitations of the analyses, the results can only be

\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

considered supportive of no detrimental OS of SG but do not support a demonstration of a survival benefit of SG.

The high proportion of patients receiving SG at PD reflects currently the recommended standard of clinical practice. Results of study ASCENT-03 demonstrated an improvement in PFS but they do not support the assumption that patients with TNBC might benefit from an OS improvement when SG is applied as first-line as opposed to later line treatment.

In view of the still high rate of censoring before month 20, the MAH committed to provide results from the final OS analysis post-approval ( REC ).

Results for secondary efficacy endpoints of ORR (48.4% versus 45.5%), time to response (2.6 vs. 2.4 months) and median TTD in the fatigue domain of the EORTC QLQ-C30 (1.5 vs 1.6 months) were similar between the SG group and TPC group. The mean change from baseline in the physical functioning (PF) domain of EORTC QLQ-C30 at Week 25 numerically favoured the SG group over the TPC group (0.3 vs. -6.6); however, the clinical relevance of the difference of 6.9 (95% CI 3.5, 10.3) is questionable, given that the minimal score change for the PF domain is 6.67, i.e. one item in the PF domain changed by one category assuming all other items were answered the same. Furthermore, the observed difference (mainly driven by the decline in the TPC group) may be biased considering also the potential impact of the open-label study design on PRO endpoints. Median DOR was numerically longer with SG compared with TPC (12.2 versus 7.2 months) in line with PFS results.

Exploratory PFS2 results indicated a trend in favour of SG (HR: 0.778 [95% CI: 0.632, 0.958]; median PFS2 16.16 months [95% CI: 14.82, 19.58] in the SG group versus 13.83 months [95% CI: 12.48, 15.84] in the TPC group.

Subgroup results of PFS generally showed a consistent benefit of SG compared to TPC, including disease stratification factors and geographic region.

For some subgroups, such as patients with stable brain metastasis (N=29), the sample size was too small to draw reliable conclusions. It is nonetheless reassuring that subgroup analyses of PFS, OS and ORR for patients with brain metastasis showed favourable results for the SG treatment arm. Systematic response assessment of brain metastasis was not conducted. MRI brain scan was mandatory at screening in all patients and follow-up was required if brain metastasis was known or suspected. But numeric measurements of the brain lesions were not collected, since brain lesions, per protocol, were captured as nontarget lesions at baseline or as new lesions at the time of progression. Reported results of 'noncomplete response/nonprogressive disease' or 'not evaluable' for patients with known brain metastasis at baseline or the proportion of patients with new brain metastases during or following treatment appear to be balanced between both treatment groups. No firm conclusions can be drawn from these results.

Subgroup results by age were provided for the cut-offs of 65 and 75 years at OS IA1 (DCO: 27 October 2025). For patients beyond 65 years (N=143), the PFS HR point estimate was numerically larger compared to younger patients (HR 0.89 [95% CI 0.58, 1.35] vs 0.61 [95% CI 0.48, 0.76]). For the age group of ≥ 65, the OS HR was 1.01 [95% CI 0.64, 1.61] and respon se rates were lower for SG versus TPC (Odds Ratio: 0.71). In the small subgroup of patients beyond 75 years (N=40), the PFS HR was 0.95 (95% CI 0.37, 2.43), the OS HR was 1.15 (95% CI 0.43, 3.05) and the Odds Ratio for ORR was 0.43 (95% CI 0.12, 1.57). Interpretation of efficacy results in elderly is limited by the small sample size and low event numbers, especially for patients above 75 years. Data are not considered reliably enough to conclude on a detriment as a basis for restricting access to treatment. Nonetheless subgroup results by age overall indicate no clinically meaningful clinical benefit for SG compared to TPC in elderly patients.

\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

Reported subgroup results for patients with BRCA1/2 mutations at baseline based on local testing (N=35 at OS IA1) did not support a PFS benefit of SG compared to TPC (PFS HR: 0.93 [95% CI 0.43, 2.02] and indicated a detriment in OS (OS HR: 2.72 [95% CI 0.95, 7.83]), although the small sample size hampered any reliable conclusions.

The MAH provided additional analyses based on retrospective central BRCA assessment: an exploratory objective of the study as per the protocol was to conduct whole exome sequencing analyses in a central laboratory. In participants with sufficient FFPE tumour material, BRCA1/BRCA2 mutation status was determined and was correlated with efficacy endpoints. For the endpoint of PFS by BICR, hazard ratios suggested a benefit of SG over TPC in both participants with BRCA1/2 mutant alleles (HR: 0.61; 95% CI: 0.34, 1.07) and participants with BRCA wild type alleles (HR: 0.70; 95% CI: 0.55, 0.91). OS HRs were overall similar between BRCA 1/2 mutant and wild type patients (OS HR: 1.08 [95% CI: 0.55, 2.14] and 0.98 [95% CI: 0.74, 1.31]).

The value of the analysis of central BRCA test results based on whole exome sequencing is limited by its exploratory and retrospective nature and the non-randomized comparisons in a Biomarker Analysis Set. Nonetheless, testing was conducted in a relevant proportion of study participants (N=423, 76%) and positive BRCA 1/2 mutations were reported in 18.2% of tested patients (N=77). Results indicated that patients with BRCA1/2 mutant or wild type alleles might derive a similar PFS benefit from SG treatment. In addition, OS data did not confirm the detriment that was observed in the analysis of local BRCA test results. Thus, despite the limitations of the dataset, it can be concluded that the treatment effect of SG might not be relevantly impacted by BRCA mutation status and a presentation of subgroup results in section 5.1 of the SmPC is not considered warranted.

Evaluation of baseline Trop-2 expression in fresh or archival tumour tissue as a biomarker for efficacy was an exploratory endpoint. Trop-2 expression in tumour tissue was assessed by IHC using a fit-for-purpose, validated EPR20043 formulation locked assay (FLA) at Roche Tissue Diagnostics (RTD). Trop-2 data were available from 499 participants (89.4%) in the ITT Analysis Set. Subgroup analysis of PFS by BICR generally showed overall a consistent benefit for the SG group compared with the TPC group across Trop-2 H-score quartiles. Subgroup results of OS showed a numerical OS benefit in the two upper Trop-2 H-score quartiles (HR point estimates: 0.61 for Q3 and 0.91 for Q4), but not in the two lower Trop-2 H-score quartiles (HR point estimates: 1.01 for Q1 and 1.37 for Q2). A similar trend was observed for ORR by BICR (odds ratio of 1.4 for Q3 and Q4 versus odds ratio of 0.99 for Q1 and 0.93 for Q2).

Trop-2 expression results need to be interpreted with caution since Trop-2 expression status was not a stratification factor and though expression data were analysed in a large proportion, they were not available for all study participants. There are wide confidence intervals for OS HRs that are likely due to small event numbers per quartile. Nonetheless, considering the mode of action of SG as a targeted therapy, an association between Trop-2 expression status and OS outcome would be biologically plausible.

## 2.4.4. Conclusions on the clinical efficacy

The ASCENT-03 study demonstrated a statistically significant improvement in PFS for SG compared to first-line chemotherapy in patients with PD-L1 negative TNBC. This improvement can also be considered clinically relevant. However, the study did not show an OS benefit, with an OS maturity rate of 55%. The interpretation of OS data is complicated by the fact that a large proportion of patients (55%) in the control arm received SG after disease progression. Nonetheless, administering SG after the failure of 1L treatment reflects standard clinical practice. The OS results can be considered sufficiently mature to exclude an OS detriment however they suggest that patients with TNBC may not

\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

benefit from an OS improvement when SG is used as first-line treatment compared to later-line treatment.

## 2.5. Clinical safety

## Introduction

Data from 551 participants treated with either SG 10 mg/kg or TPC chemotherapy (paclitaxel 90 mg/m2, nab paclitaxel 100 mg/m2, or gemcitabine 1000 mg/m2 with carboplatin AUC 2) in the pivotal phase 3 Study GS-US-592-6238 were integrated in the summary of clinical safety (SCS).

Pooled data from 4 clinical studies (GS-US-592-6238, IMMU-132-01, IMMU-132-05, and IMMU-13209) in 963 participants with metastatic TNBC or HR+/HER2- metastatic breast cancer (mBC) are used for the analysis of SG monotherapy safety in patients with breast cancer. Pooled data from 7 clinical studies (studies GS US-592-6238, IMMU-132-01, IMMU 132 05, IMMU-13206 (Cohort  1 and Cohort 2), IMMU-132-09, IMMU-132-13, and GS-US-577-6153) in 1999 participants across different tumour types were used to contextualize the experience of SG monotherapy in first line metastatic TNBC with the overall SG treatment experience.

All Treated (Expanded) SG (10 mg/kg) group includes all participants treated with SG 10 mg/kg regardless of tumor type in IMMU-132-01 and all participants treated with SG from IMMU-132-05, IMMU-132-06 Cohorts 1 &amp; 2, IMMU-132-09, IMMU-132-13, GS-US-577-6153, and GS-US-592-6238.

Participants treated with SG in GS-US-592-6238 crossover phase are excluded from Overall mBC and All Treated (Expanded) SG (10 mg/kg) groups.

On treatment participants are those still on treatment (randomized treatment for GS-US-592-6238) by the date of clinical database cut-off.

One participant from Study IMMU-132-01 who received study drug was mistakenly marked as 'study drug not administered after randomization'.

Patient demographics and baseline characteristics were previously presented in section 552.4.2.

AE = adverse event; CTCAE = Common Terminology Criteria for Adverse Events; ISS = Integrated Summary of Safety; mBC = metastatic breast cancer; SAE = serious adverse event; SG = sacituzumab govitecan;

TEAE = treatment-emergent adverse events; TPC = treatment of physician's choice

a AEs leading to study drug dose reduction were not collected in IMMU-132-01; therefore, IMMU-132-01 participants were excluded from the denominators for percentages of TEAEs leading to study drug dose reduction for the last 2 columns.

Percentages are based on the total number of participants in the corresponding column.

Treatment-emergent AEs are defined as any AEs that started on or after the first dose date up to and including 30 days after the last dose date or the day prior to initiation of subsequent anticancer therapy (including crossover treatment), whichever occurred first.

Treatment-related includes 'Related', 'Likely Related', 'Possibly Related', and missing relation. It excluded 'Unlikely Related', 'Not Related', and 'Unrelated'. Severity grades were defined by CTCAE: 1 = Mild; 2 = Moderate; 3 = Severe;

4 = Life-Threatening; 5 = Death.

## Adverse Drug Reactions (ADR) in the SmPC

Section 4.8 of the SmPC is updated to include the GS-US-592-6238 (ASCENT-03) study population of patients with unresectable locally advanced or metastatic triple-negative breast cancer whose tumours do not express PD-L1 or patients previously treated with anti-PD-(L)1 agents in the early setting whose tumours do express PD-L1 receiving SG as monotherapy, into the current 'Overall mBC SG' pooled safety dataset.

The below encompasses the adverse reactions included in Table 3 'List of adverse reactions' of the SmPC with related frequency categories from the latest monotherapy safety pooled dataset 'Overall mBC SG' studies (N= 963) as follows:

- Study IMMU-132-01 a
- Study IMMU-132-05 b
- Study IMMU-132-09 b
- Study GS-US-592-6238 b .
- a Only participants who received a starting dose of SG 10 mg/kg monotherapy were included
- b All SG-treated participants from the study were included in the pooled analysis

exfoliative generalised, drug hypersensitivity, infusion related hypersensitivity reaction, rash macular, rash maculo-papular, rash papular, rash pustular, rhinitis allergic, swelling face, swelling of eyelid, swollen tongue, Type IV hypersensitivity reaction, cough with pruritus, cough with rash, dyspnoea with pruritus, dyspnoea with rash, dyspnoea with wheezing and rash, hypotension with dyspnoea

7: Includes the following preferred terms: dyspnoea; dyspnoea exertional

8: Includes the preferred term of neutropenic colitis and events reported as typhlitis

9: Includes the following preferred terms: fatigue, asthenia

As a consequence of the updated 'Overall mBC SG' pooled safety dataset, the frequency of the following ADRs has been revised in section 4.8 of the SmPC as follows:

- 'Insomnia' from 'Very common' to 'Common'
- 'Stomatitis' from 'Common' to 'Very common'
- 'Blood alkaline phosphatase increased' from 'Common' to 'Very common'
- 'Infusion related reaction' from 'Uncommon' to 'Common'.

AE = adverse event; ISS = Integrated Summary of Safety; mBC = metastatic breast cancer; MedDRA = Medical Dictionary for Regulatory Activities; PT = preferred term; SG = sacituzumab govitecan; TPC = treatment of physician's choice

Treatment-emergent AEs are defined as any AEs that started on or after the first dose date up to and including 30 days after the last dose date or the day prior to initiation of subsequent anticancer therapy (including crossover treatment), whichever occurred first.

Multiple AEs are counted only once per participant for each PT.

MedDRA Version 27.1 was used for coding.

PTs were presented by descending order of the frequency in the GS-US-592-6238 SG group.

The following terms are mapped: Neutrophil count decreased à Neutropenia, White blood cell count decreased à Leukopenia, Lymphocyte count decreased à Lymphopenia, Haemoglobin decreased à Anaemia, Red blood cell count decreased à Anaemia, Platelet count decreased à Thrombocytopenia, Asthenia à Fatigue, Typhlitis à Neutropenic colitis, Dyspnoea exertional à Dyspnoea.

Fatal infections secondary to neutropenia (including neutropenic colitis) occurred in 0.6% of patients in the Overall mBC pool.

## Other significant events Adverse Events of Special Interest (AESI)

Table 61 Definition of Adverse Events of Special Interest

| AESI Category                               | Definition                                                                                                |
|---------------------------------------------|-----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|
| Serious infections secondary to neutropenia | PTs under SOC: Infection and Infestations ª (Serious infections occurring after any grade AE Neutropenia) |
| Severe diarrhea                             | PT: Diarrhea (Grade 3 or higher)                                                                          |
| Hypersensitivity b                          | PTs under: Hypersensitivity SMQ (narrow) and Anaphylactic Reaction SMQ (broad and narrow) c               |
| Neutropenia d                               | PTs: neutropenia, neutrophil count decreased, febrile neutropenia                                         |

AE = Adverse Events; AESI = Adverse Events of Special Interest; MedDRA = Medical Dictionary for Regulatory Activities; PT = Preferred Term; SMQ = Standardised MedDRA Query; SOC = System Organ Class

- a SAEs from SOC of Infections and Infestations that occurred within 11 days of neutropenia, febrile neutropenia or neutrophil count decreased.
- b For the category of Hypersensitivity, only events whose onset dates are on the day of or 1 day after study drug administration are included.
- c Anaphylactic Reaction SMQ (broad and narrow) is an algorithmic SMQ.

d Grouped AE terms

All AESI definitions are based on MedDRA version 27.1 or higher.

Treatment-emergent AEs are defined as any AEs that started on or after the first dose date up to and including 30 days after the last dose date or the day prior to initiation of subsequent anticancer therapy (including crossover treatment), whichever occurred first.

Treatment-related includes 'Related', 'Likely Related', 'Possibly Related', and missing relation. It excluded 'Unlikely Related', 'Not Related', and 'Unrelated'. Severity grades were defined by CTCAE: 1 = Mild; 2 = Moderate; 3 = Severe;

4 = Life-Threatening; 5 = Death.

See definitions of Adverse Events of Special Interest in the Statistical Analysis Plan, CSR, and/or Summary of Clinical Safety.

## Time to Onset and Duration of Serious Infections Secondary to Neutropenia

The median time to first onset of serious infections secondary to neutropenia was similar in the study GS -US -592 -6238 SG group (20 days) compared with the All Treated (Expanded) SG pool (17 days). The median time to onset of the first event of Grade 3 or higher serious infections secondary to neutropenia in the study GS -US -592 -6238 SG group (45 days) was longer than in the All Treated (Expanded) SG pool (17 days) (GS-US-592-6238 ISS [Expanded]).

The median durations of serious infections secondary to neutropenia of any grade and Grade 3 or higher serious infections secondary to neutropenia were longer in the study GS-US-592-6238 SG group (13 and 12 days, respectively) than in the All Treated (Expanded) SG pool (7 days and 7 days, respectively).

## Time to Onset and Duration of Severe Diarrhoea

The median time from first dose of the study drug to onset of the first event of severe (Grade 3 or higher) diarrhoea was longer in the study GS -US -592 -6238 SG group (67 days) than in the All Treated (Expanded) SG (15 days) pool.

The median duration of severe diarrhoea was similar between the study GS -US -592 -6238 SG group (6 days) and the All Treated (Expanded) SG (7 days) pool (GS-US-592-6238 ISS [Expanded]).

## Time to Onset and Duration of Neutropenia

The median time to first onset of any grade neutropenia and first onset of Grade 3 or higher neutropenia were longer in the study GS -US -592 -6238 SG group (both 22 days) compared to the All Treated (Expanded) SG pool (both 15 days). The median time to onset of neutropenia (including febrile neutropenia) following the start of the first treatment cycle was 20 days in the Overall mBC pool.  Median duration of any grade neutropenia was 10 days in the Overall mBC pool.

## Management of Neutropenia

G-CSF use at any time during the study was higher in the study GS-US-592-6238 SG group than in the All Treated (Expanded) SG pool (58.9% vs 46.0%, respectively). These results suggest the increased use of G-CSF in study GS-US-592-6238 and adherence to protocol-specified guidance for the management of neutropenia. G-CSF use in the study GS-US-592-6238 SG group for both treatment and prophylaxis was also higher than those in the All Treated (Expanded) SG pool (treatment: 37.5% vs 29.9%; prophylaxis: 44.0% vs 26.6%).

However, then an update of protocol specified guidance for the management of neutropenia (G-CSF use) was implemented late in this study, in November 2024. Therefore, this change most likely had no further effect on the severity and number of infections in this study.

Severity grades were defined by the CTCAE Version 5.0.

For maximum postbaseline toxicity grade, the most severe graded abnormality from all tests was counted for each participant.

For each individual laboratory test, the most severe graded abnormality for that test was counted for a participant.

A TE laboratory abnormality was defined as an increase of at least 1 toxicity grade from baseline at any time postbaseline up to and including the date of last study drug dose plus 30 days or the day prior to initiation of subsequent anticancer therapy (including crossover treatment), whichever occurs first. If the baseline laboratory value is missing, any abnormality of at least Grade 1 observed within the time frame specified above (TE window) will be considered TE. Percentages were based on participant with nonmissing postbaseline values within the TE window.

## Safety in special populations

## Race

Analysis of the safety data in the racial subgroups include the following findings:

- Grade 3 or higher AEs were lower in the GS-US-592-6238 SG group versus the All Treated (Expanded) SG Pool in White participants (63.4% vs 72.8%) and Asian participants (65.2% vs 77.4%), whereas the reverse was true in Black or African American participants (88.9% vs 74.3%).
- SAEs were lower in the GS-US-592-6238 SG group versus the All Treated (Expanded) SG Pool in White participants (26.3% vs 38.6%), Asian participants (21.2% vs 34.4%), and Other participants (32.0% vs 40.3%) and were similar in Black or African American participants (33.3% vs 31.4%).
- AEs leading to study drug dose reduction were higher in the GS-US-592-6238 SG group versus the All Treated (Expanded) SG Pool in Black or African American participants (55.6% vs 35.3%).
- AEs leading to study drug discontinuation were lower in the GS-US-592-6238 SG group versus the All Treated (Expanded) SG Pool in White participants (3.4% vs 8.6%), Black or African American participants (0 vs 4.3%), Asian participants (4.5% vs 9.4%), and Other participants (4.0% vs 10.7%).
- Rates of AEs leading to death were low across racial subgroups, with no notable trends comparing the GS-US-592-6238 SG group versus the All Treated (Expanded) SG Pool.
- Interpretation is limited in the Black or African American, American Indian or Alaska Native, and Other race subgroups given then low number of participants.

## Age

Median age was similar in the All Treated (Expanded) SG pool and the original All Treated SG pool (61 and 58 years, respectively). Median age was lower in the study GS -US -592 -6238 SG group (56 years) compared to the All Treated (Expanded) SG pool (61 years). In all populations, most participants were &lt; 65 years of age.

Median duration of treatment was consistent between the All Treated (Expanded) SG Pool and the original All Treated SG pool within age subgroups. The median duration of SG treatment was longer in the study GS -US -592 -6238 SG group than in the All Treated (Expanded) SG pool in participants &lt; 65 and ≥ 65 years of age but was the same in participants ≥ 75 years of age. (GS-US-592-6238 ISS [Expanded],

- &lt; 65 years: 8.79 months (SG group; n = 212) and 4.60 months (All Treated [Expanded] SG Pool; n = 1209)
- ≥ 65 years: 6.97 months (SG group; n = 63) and 3.71 months (All Treated [Expanded] SG Pool; n = 790)
- ≥ 75 years: 3.14 months (SG group; n = 20), and 3.14 months (All Treated [Expanded] SG Pool; n = 232).

Within the study GS -US -592 -6238 SG group, median duration of treatment was longer in the &lt; 65 years subgroup than in the ≥ 65 years and ≥ 75 subgroups, with the ≥ 75 subgroup having a greater magnitude of difference.

\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

The incidence of SAEs in the study GS -US -592 -6238 SG group was also higher in the ≥ 75 years and ≥ 65 years subgroup than in the &lt; 65 years subgroup. A similar trend was seen in the All Treated (Expanded) SG Pool:

- &lt; 65 years: 21.7% (SG group) and 32.9% (All Treated [Expanded] SG Pool)
- ≥ 65 years: 39.7% (SG group) and 46.2% (All Treated [Expanded] SG Pool)
- ≥ 75 years: 50% (SG group) and 55.2% (All Treated [Expanded] SG Pool).

A similar trend was also seen in Grade 3 or higher AEs in the All Treated (Expanded) SG Pool.

The incidence of any AEs leading to discontinuation in the study GS -US -592 -6238 SG group was higher in the ≥ 75 years and ≥ 65 years subgroups than in the &lt; 65 years subgroup. A similar trend was seen in the All Treated (Expanded) SG Pool:

- &lt; 65 years: 2.4% (SG group) and 5.8% (All Treated [Expanded] SG Pool)
- ≥ 65 years: 7.9% (SG group) and 13.5% (All Treated [Expanded] SG Pool)
- ≥ 75 years: 25.0% (SG group) and 15.5% (All Treated [Expanded] SG Pool).

Treatment-emergent AEs are defined as any AEs that started on or after the first dose date up to and including 30 days after the last dose date or the day prior to initiation of subsequent anticancer therapy (including crossover treatment), whichever occurred first.

Severity grades were defined by CTCAE: 1 = Mild; 2 = Moderate; 3 = Severe; 4 = Life-Threatening; 5 = Death.

## Serious Adverse Events

In the study GS-US-592-6238 SG group, SAEs were reported in a larger proportion of participants who were heterozygous for the UGT1A1*28 allele compared with participants who were homozygous for the UGT1A1*28 allele or the UGT1A1*1 allele (27.2% vs 21.7%, and 20.7%, respectively).

Neutropenia, febrile neutropenia, and diarrhoea were the only SAEs that occurred in ≥ 3.0% of participants in any genotype:

- UGT1A1*1/*1: febrile neutropenia (3.6%)
- UGT1A1*1/*28: diarrhea (4.9%), febrile neutropenia (3.9%), neutropenia (3.9%)
- UGT1A1*28/*28: neutropenia (4.3%), diarrhea (4.3%), neutropenic colitis (4.3%), colitis (4.3%), stomatitis (4.3%), pneumonia bacterial (4.3%), erysipelas (4.3%)
- UGT1A1*1/*6: febrile neutropenia (7.7%), septic shock (7.7%), herpes zoster (7.7%), pleural effusion (7.7%), dyspnea (7.7%), acute respiratory failure (7.7%).

Serious AEs were reported in a larger proportion of participants who were homozygous for the UGT1A1*28 allele compared with participants who were either heterozygous for the UGT1A1*28 allele or homozygous for the UGT1A1*1 allele in the Overall mBC pool (39.4% vs 28.3% and 23.5%, respectively) and the All Treated (Expanded) SG pool (50.0% vs 35.7% and 33.6%, respectively.

## Safety related to drug-drug interactions and other interactions

General considerations made about DDI for SG are valid also for this extension of indication. No dedicated DDI studies have been performed.

## Adverse Events Leading to Dose Reduction

In study GS-US-592-6238, the incidence of AEs leading to dose reduction of SG or TPC was lower in the SG group than in the TPC group (36.7% vs 44.9%).

The most commonly reported ( ≥ 5% of participants in either treatment group) AEs leading to dose reduction of SG or TPC by treatment were as follows:

- SG: neutropenia (17.5%) and diarrhea (5.5%)
- TPC: neutropenia (19.2%), thrombocytopenia (7.6%), and anemia (6.9%).

The most commonly reported AEs leading to SG dose reduction in the study GS-US-592-6238 SG group were similar to those in the Overall mBC and All Treated SG pools.

## Adverse Events Leading to Study Drug Interruption

In study GS-US-592-6238, the incidence of AEs leading to interruption of any study drug was similar between the SG group and the TPC group (65.8% vs 62.0%).

The most commonly reported AEs leading to interruption of any study drug by treatment group were as follows:

\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

- SG: neutropenia (43.3%), diarrhoea (5.8%), leukopenia (5.1%), and COVID 19 (5.1%)
- TPC: neutropenia (37.0%), thrombocytopenia (18.8%), and anaemia (13.4%).

Neutropenia was also the most frequently reported AE leading to study drug interruption in the Overall mBC and All Treated SG pools.

## Post marketing experience

Cumulative patient exposure to Trodelvy from the first marketing approval in the US on 22 April 2020 up to 21 April 2025 is estimated to be 66,804 patients, including 21,518 patients in the EU.

## 2.5.1. Discussion on clinical safety

The safety population of interest are patients from the pivotal study GS-US-592-6238 (n=275 who received SG). Additional Safety data has been provided for 963 patients that received SG 10 mg/kg in the metastatic breast cancer pool (mBC pool). The size of the safety data available in breast cancer patients who have received SG at the proposed dose of 10 mg/kg on Days 1 and 8 Q3W is considered acceptable. In addition, data of an expanded all treated population (including data from metastatic Urothelial Carcinoma (mUC) and metastatic Non Small Cell Lung Cancer (mNSCLC) trials) were provided. The median treatment duration was longer in the SG arm than in the TPC arm i.e. 8.31 months (range: 0.03, 28.68) vs 5.78 months (range: 0.03, 24.18). Approximately 60% of the patients in the SG arm were treated for more than 6 months; however, only half of them for more than 12 months. The dose of SG was reduced in approximately 36% of patients; therefore, the relative dose intensity was above 90% in only about 50% of the patients.

Almost all of the patients experienced at least one adverse event (AE) in the pivotal study GS-US592-6238 and the most frequently observed adverse events in the SG arm vs the TPC arm were neutropenia (66.5% vs 56.9%), nausea (60.7% vs 34.4%) and alopecia (54.8% vs 27.2%), diarrhoea (53.8% vs 19.9%) and fatigue (47.3% vs 46.7%).

Treatment-related AEs (ADRs) with SG vs TPC were also neutropenia (66.2% vs 56.2%), nausea (58.2% vs 26.6%), alopecia (54.2% vs 26.8%) and diarrhoea (56.7% vs 16.9%). In general, Gastrointestinal toxicity was markedly increased with SG vs TPC, whereas haematological toxicities occurred in both treatment arms.

Grade ≥3 AEs in the SG arm vs the TPC arm were: neutropenia (42.9% and 40.6.0%), diarrhoea (9.1% vs 0.7%), leukopenia (7.3% vs 12.7%), anaemia (4.4% and 16.1%) and febrile neutropenia (4.4% vs 1.1%). Treatmentrelated grade ≥3 adverse events were more frequent in the SG arm compared to the TPC arm (60.7% vs. 53.3%).

Adverse events of special interest (AESI) included neutropenia (including febrile neutropenia), infections secondary to neutropenia, diarrhoea and hypersensitivity, which are important known risks of treatment with SG.

Neutropenia was as expected commonly observed in both the pivotal study GS US592 6238 (68.0%), the mBC pool (68.8%) and the All Treated (Expanded) SG population (59.3%). These events were often of grade 3 or higher (45.1% vs 40.9%) in the study GS-US-592-6238. The majority of these events were assessed to be treatment-related and often led to study drug interruptions and reductions. G-CSF use at any time during the study was higher in the study GS-US-592-6238 SG group than in the All Treated (Expanded) SG pool (58.9% vs 46.0%, respectively). These results could suggest the increased use of G-CSF in study GS-US-592-6238, reflecting the better knowledge of neutropenia and management of neutropenia, as a common adverse event for SG. Protocol specified guidance for the

\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

management of neutropenia was changed with Amendment 4 (dated November 2024). This late implementation does not suggest an additional effect on incidences and severity of neutropenia in study GS-US-592-6238. Febrile neutropenia was observed as grade 3 or higher and the incidences were similar in the SG arm, the mBC pool and the All Treated (Expanded) SG population, 4.4% vs 5.6 vs 7.1% respectively.

Adverse events and treatment-related AEs of severe diarrhoea occurred almost exclusively in participants in the SG group (9.1% and 7.6%, respectively) compared to the TPC group (0.7% and 0.4%, respectively) in study GS-US-592-6238. One participant (0.4%) discontinued SG treatment in study GS-US-592-6238 because of diarrhoea. Incidences and severity of diarrhoea are similar in the SG arm compared to the mBC pool and the All Treated (Expanded) SG population.

Serious Adverse events (SAEs) were observed with 25.8% in the SG arm compared to 24.3% in the TPC arm, which is similar to the incidences observed in the mBC pool (27.6%) and lower compared to the All Treated (Expanded) SG population (38.2%). The most commonly observed SAEs in the SG arm, the mBC pool and the All Treated (Expanded) SG Population were febrile neutropenia (3.6% vs 4.5% vs. 6.0%), neutropenia (3.6% vs 2.9% vs 3.7%, respectively), diarrhoea (3.3% vs 3.7% vs 5.0%, respectively) and pneumonia (2.2% vs 2.1% vs 2.5%, respectively).

Remarkably, approximately 10% of SAEs are infections and infestations and approximately 2% of patients experienced sepsis or a septic shock. In the TPC arm the most common SAEs were pneumonia (3.3%) and neutropenia (2.9%).

In the SG arm in GS-US-592-6238, 36.7% had died: 29.8% of disease progression and 3.3% of an adverse event. In comparison, more patients had died in the TPC arm with 37.0%; 32.2% died of disease progression and 1.1% died from an adverse event. Hence, treatment with SG seems to carry more severe toxicity, which is outweighed by fewer cases of disease progression and death compared to standard of care (TPC). Causes of death were mostly infections (sepsis, pneumonia).

In study GS-US-592-6238, 3.6% of the patients in the SG arm discontinued treatment due to adverse events, which is significantly lower compared to the TPC arm (12.0%) and comparable to the rate for the mBC pool (4.7%) and lower compared to the All Treated Population (6.9%). Discontinuations rates appear really low, taking the high SAE rates into consideration.

Dose reductions of SG were common and the rates were similar in both the SG arm (36.7%) and the TPC arm (44.9%). Most frequent AEs leading to dose reductions were neutropenia and diarrhoea, which is consistent with the known safety profile of SG.

Dose interruptions were similar in the SG arm (65.8%) and the TPC arm (62%). Most frequent AEs leading to dose interruptions were neutropenia, diarrhoea, leukopenia, and COVID19. The rate of dose reductions and dose interruptions with SG are high but acceptable considering the targeted noncurative setting.

Laboratory findings were in line with the reported AEs.

Available data in the elderly population (&gt;65 years) are limited especially in over 75 years. Increasing toxicity of SG could be observed with increasing age regarding serious adverse events (SAEs) both in the GS-US-592-6238 study, the mBC pool and the All Treated (Expanded) SG Population. In GS-US-592-6238 study, the incidences of grade ≥ 3, SAEs and deaths were 81.0%, 39.7% and 6.3%, respectively, in patients 65 years or older compared to 61.3%, 21.7% and 1.4%, respectively, in patients younger than 65 years which raises concerns about the B/R in the elderly patient population. In addition, the tolerability of the chemotherapy regimen of the TPC arm in the elderly population with 66.7% of Grade ≥ 3 AEs, 24.4% of SAEs and no deaths does not indicate a significantly worse safety profile of this regimen in the elderly population. The higher discontinuation

\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

rate due to ADRs, the increased risk of febrile neutropenia, severe diarrhoea and fatal infections in older patients are now reflected in section 4.8 of the SmPC.

SN-38 (the small molecule moiety of sacituzumab govitecan) is metabolised via UGT1A1. The UGT1A1 *28 allele is associated with decreased rates of transcription, initiation, expression, and enzyme activity of UDP-glucuronosyltransferase 1-1. With a decrease in enzyme activity, SN-38 metabolism and hence, detoxification, is reduced and the exposure time of active SN-38 in the intestines is prolonged. Individuals who are homozygous for the UGT1A1*28 allele are potentially at increased risk for toxicities from SG. Approximately 20% of the Black or African American population, 10% of the White population, and 2% of the East Asian population are homozygous for the UGT1A1*28 allele. Incidences of AEs were high in patients regardless of genotype, however, Grade 3 or higher AEs were reported in a significantly higher percentage of participants who were homozygous for the UGT1A1*28 allele compared with participants who were either heterozygous or were homozygous for the UGT1A1*1 allele in the mBC pool and the All Treated (Expanded) SG Population (83.0%/81.5% vs 72.5%/73.8% vs 67.2%/68.7%). This applies also to the serious AEs which were reported in half of participants who were homozygous for the UGT1A1*28 allele (50.0%) compared with participants who were either heterozygous (35.7%) or homozygous for the UGT1A1*1 allele (33.6%) in the All Treated (Expanded) SG Population.

Approximately 0.2% of the Black population, 0.3% of the White population, and 27% of the East Asian population are heterozygous for the UGT1A1*6 allele. Data were provided for SAEs and the UGT1A1*6 allele where notably high incidences of febrile neutropenia (7.7%), septic shock (7.7%), herpes zoster (7.7%), pleural effusion (7.7%), dyspnoea (7.7%), acute respiratory failure (7.7%) were reported in GS-US-592-6238 study. The genotype UGT1A1*1/*6 seems to be associated with increased risk of ADRs comparable to the risk for UGT1A1*28/*28 genotype (52% SAEs, 20% drug discontinuations, 10% death). Although information is limited based on the sample size of UGT1A1*1/*6 genotypes, there is a biological rationale for increased safety risks for patients with this genotype.

The higher incidence of Grade 3-4 neutropenia, febrile neutropenia, and anaemia observed in patients homozygous for the UGT1A1*28 allele and patients heterozygous for the UGT1A1*6 allele compared to patients homozygous for the wild-type allele has been reflected in sections 4.4 and 4.8 of the SmPC.

## 2.5.2. Conclusions on clinical safety

The overall toxicity observed with SG 10 mg/kg is considered manageable and in line with what has previously been observed in other clinical trials and the incidences reflected in the mBC pool and the All Treated Expanded SG population. No new toxicities were observed in the pivotal study GS-US-5926238. The safety profile of SG is considered rather unfavourable compared to the standard chemotherapy arm mainly due to fatal events secondary to neutropenia and gastrointestinal disorders (severe diarrhoea); nonetheless, toxicities can be regarded as manageable by support with G-CSF and dose modifications and overall the safety profile of SG can be considered acceptable in the proposed indication.

## 2.5.3. PSUR cycle

The requirements for submission of periodic safety update reports for this medicinal product are set out in the list of Union reference dates (EURD list) provided for under Article 107c (7) of Directive 2001/83/EC and any subsequent updates published on the European medicines web-portal.

## 2.6. Risk management plan

The MAH submitted an updated RMP version with this application.

## 2.7. Update of the Product information

As a consequence of this new indication, sections 4.1, 4.2, 4.4, 4.5, 4.8, 5.1 and 5.2 of the SmPC have been updated. The Package Leaflet has been updated accordingly.

Changes were also made to the PI to bring it in line with the current Agency/QRD template, which were reviewed and accepted by the CHMP.

In addition, the MAH took the opportunity to make corrections to the BG, CZ, DE, DK, EE, ES, FI, FR, HR, HU, IT, IS, LT, LV, MT, NL, NO, PL, PT, SE, SK, SV translations.

## 2.7.1. User consultation

A justification for not performing a full user consultation with target patient groups on the package leaflet has been submitted by the MAH and has been found acceptable for the following reasons: the package leaflet has been amended to reflect the new indication for the treatment of first-line metastatic TNBC in adult patients but the updates are considered slight. The key safety messages as well as the design and layout of the package leaflet are not significantly affected.

## 3. Benefit-Risk Balance

## 3.1. Therapeutic Context

The recommended indication reflecting the data evaluated is:

Trodelvy is indicated as monotherapy for the treatment of adult patients with unresectable locally advanced or metastatic triple-negative breast cancer (TNBC) who have not received prior systemic therapy for metastatic disease and who are not candidates for PD-1 or PD-L1 inhibitor therapy (see section 5.1).

## 3.1.1. Disease or condition

TNBC is defined by a lack of ER, PR, and HER2 expression in tumour cells, and accounts for approximately 15% of invasive breast cancers. It is characterized by a more aggressive tumour biology and shorter overall survival with a low 5-year survival rate of approximately 12% (American Cancer Society 2023 12 ). Approximately 33% of patients with metastatic TNBC present with de novo metastatic disease (Punie et al. 2024 13 ), and TNBC is most commonly associated with visceral metastases affecting the liver, lung, and brain (O'Reilly et al. 2021 14 ).

## 3.1.2. Available therapies and unmet medical need

Current standard treatment for recurrent/metastatic TNBC varies by subtypes. For patients with de novo metastatic TNBC or recurrence of &gt;6-12 months after the end of (neo)adjuvant immune checkpoint inhibitors (ICI) most recent ESMO guidance recommends a combination of chemotherapy with an ICI for PD-L1 positive patients. PD-L1 negative patients with germline BRCA 1/2 mutations should be treated with a PARP inhibitor and gBRCA-wt PD-L1 negative patients should receive chemotherapy (ESMO living guideline 15 ).

12  American Cancer Society. Triple-negative Breast Cancer. Available at: https://www.cancer.org/cancer/types/breast-

cancer/about/types-of-breast-cancer/triple-negative.html. Last Updated: 01 March.  2023

13  Punie K et al. Evolution of characteristics, real-world treatment landscape, and survival outcomes in patients with previously untreated mTNBC in the United States [Abstract]. ESMO Breast Cancer; 2024

14  O'Reilly D et al. Overview of recent advances in metastatic triple negative breast cancer. World J Clin Oncol 2021 15  ESMO Living Guideline for Triple-negative metastatic breast cancer; G Curigliano et al., Ann Oncol. 2025.

\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

The subset of patients with PD L1 positive metastatic TNBC (CPS ≥ 10) accounts for approximately 40% of patients with metastatic TNBC and the prevalence of germline BRCA mutations is only 9.3% to 15.4% among patients with TNBC (Armstrong et al. 2019). For the majority of patients, first-line treatment remains chemotherapy which is associated with a short median PFS of 5.6 months and median OS of 16 months (Cortes et al. 2022). Therefore, there is still significant unmet need for treatments for the subgroup of patients with TNBC who are not candidates for ICI (or PARPi).

## 3.1.3. Main clinical studies

Study ASCENT-03 is a global, open-label phase 3 study to evaluate the efficacy and safety of SG compared with TPC chemotherapy in participants with locally advanced unresectable or metastatic TNBC who have not received previous systemic therapy for advanced disease. The study enrolled participants with tumours that were PD-L1 negative (CPS &lt; 10) and planned to recruit participants with PD-L1 positive tumours if they previously received a PD-1 or PD-L1 inhibitor in the (neo)adjuvant or had a comorbidity precluding treatment with a PD-(L)1inhibitor.

558 participants were randomized 1:1 to receive SG (10 mg/kg D1 and D8, Q3W) or TPC (paclitaxel, nab-paclitaxel or gemcitabine plus carboplatin). All but two patients had PD-L1 negative tumours. The primary endpoint was PFS as assessed by BICR. Participants in the control group were eligible to receive SG through the study in the crossover phase upon disease progression verified by BICR.

## 3.2. Favourable effects

At the time of the primary analysis of PFS (DCO: 02 April 2025 with a median duration of follow-up of 13.2 months), the ASCENT-03 study demonstrated a statistically significant PFS improvement of SG compared to TPC (HR: 0.623; 95% CI: 0.501, 0.775; 2-sided P &lt; 0.0001). The median PFS was 9.69 months (95% CI: 8.15, 11.14) in the SG group and 6.93 months (95% CI: 5.62, 8.25) in the TPC group. Sensitivity analysis of PFS were consistent with the primary analysis.

OS data from the first OS interim analysis based on an OS maturity rate of 50.7% (DCO: 27 October 2025 with a median duration of follow-up of 17.7 months) showed similar results in both treatment arms (HR: 0.953; 95% CI: 0.755, 1.205, P = 0.6860). The median OS was 21.45 months (95% CI: 18.86, 26.55) in the SG group and 21.26 months (95% CI: 18.69, 25.17) in the TPC group. The OS KM curves largely overlapped until month 24 when they began to separate in favour of SG. Of note, numbers of events beyond 24 months were limited.

## 3.3. Uncertainties and limitations about favourable effects

- Interpretation of OS data is hampered by the high cross-over rate; at OS IA1, 55.2% participants in the TPC group received SG in the second line setting, 44.8% received SG through the crossover phase of the study. Data in elderly are limited, especially in patients older than 75 years.
- The study population did not represent patients with PD-L1 positive disease who have a comorbidity precluding treatment with ICI or cannot be rechallenged with ICI upon disease recurrence. Nonetheless, it can be assumed that tumour PD-L1 expression is not an effect modifier and that study results from ASCENT-03 are also relevant for these PD-L1 positive patients for whom chemotherapy would be the choice of therapy, therefore the final indication reflects the extended population of patients who are not candidates for PD-1 or PD-L1 inhibitor therapy. The study population characteristics are presented in section 5.1 of the SmPC.

\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_\\_

<div style=\"page-break-after: always\"></div>

## 3.4. Unfavourable effects

Common AEs observed in the GS-US-592-6238 study in the SG arm were neutropenia (66.5%), nausea (60.7%), alopecia (54.9%), diarrhoea (53.8%) and fatigue (47.3%). Grade ≥ 3 AEs were neutropenia (42.9%), diarrhoea (9.1%), leukopenia (7.3%), anaemia (4.4%) and febrile neutropenia (4.4%) Adverse events of special interest (AESI) included neutropenia (including febrile neutropenia), infections secondary to neutropenia, diarrhoea and hypersensitivity.

Serious adverse events (SAEs) were observed with 25.8% in the SG arm in the GS-US-592-6238 study. The most commonly observed SAEs in the SG arm were febrile neutropenia (3.6%), neutropenia (3.6%), diarrhoea (3.3%) and pneumonia (2.2%).

In the GS-US-592-6238 study, 3.3% of patients in the SG arm died due to an AE.

## 3.5. Uncertainties and limitations about unfavourable effects

Data in elderly are limited, especially in patients older than 75 years. Higher incidences of febrile neutropenia, severe diarrhoea, fatal infections and discontinuations due to adverse reactions were observed in patients aged 65 years or older compared with younger patients with metastatic breast cancer. There was a higher incidence rate of serious adverse events in patients aged 75 years or older (50.0%) compared to patients aged 65 years or older (39.7%) and patients younger than 65 years (21.7%) in the GS-US-592-6238 study.

## 3.7. Benefit-risk assessment and discussion

## 3.7.1. Importance of favourable and unfavourable effects

The ASCENT-03 study demonstrated a statistically significant improvement in PFS for SG compared to first-line chemotherapy in patients with PD-L1 negative TNBC. This improvement can also be considered clinically relevant. However, the study did not show an OS benefit, with an OS maturity rate of 55%. The interpretation of OS data is complicated by the fact that a large proportion of patients (55%) in the control arm received SG after disease progression. Nonetheless, administering SG after the failure of 1L treatment reflects standard clinical practice. The OS data can be considered sufficiently mature to rule out a detrimental effect; however they do not demonstrate an OS benefit in patients with TNBC when SG is administered in the first-line setting compared with later-lines of treatment.

The overall safety profile of SG in ASCENT-03 study is in line with what has been observed previously with SG. No new ADRs and no new risks have been identified in the RMP. The safety profile of SG is considered rather unfavourable compared to the chemotherapeutic agents of the control arm, mainly due to high rates of infections secondary to neutropenia (e.g. sepsis) and gastrointestinal disorders (e.g. severe diarrhoea); nonetheless, toxicities can be regarded as manageable by support with G-CSF and dose modifications.

## 3.7.2. Balance of benefits and risks

In view of the demonstrated benefit in PFS for SG and its manageable safety profile, SG can be considered a viable treatment option for first-line therapy in patients with TNBC.

## 3.7.3. Additional considerations on the benefit-risk balance

N/A

## 3.8. Conclusions

The overall B/R of Trodelvy in the new claimed indication is positive.

## 4. Recommendations

## Outcome

Based on the review of the submitted data, the CHMP considers the following variation acceptable and therefore recommends the variation to the terms of the Marketing Authorisation, concerning the following change:

| Variation accepted   | Variation accepted                                                                  | Type              | Annexes affected   |
|----------------------|-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|-------------------|--------------------|
| C.I.6.a              | C.I.6.a Addition of a new therapeutic indication or modification of an approved one | Variation type II | I and IIIB         |

Extension of indication for Trodelvy as monotherapy for the treatment of adult patients with unresectable locally advanced or metastatic triple-negative breast cancer (TNBC) who have not received prior systemic therapy for metastatic disease and who are not candidates for PD-1 or PD-L1 inhibitor therapy, based on the results from study GS-US-592-6238 (ASCENT-03), which is a phase 3 study of sacituzumab govitecan (IMMU-132) versus treatment of physician's choice (TPC) in patients with previously untreated, locally advanced, inoperable or metastatic triple-negative breast cancer whose tumours do not express PD-L1 or in patients previously treated with anti-PD-(L)1 agents in the early setting whose tumours do express PD-L1. As a consequence, sections 4.1, 4.2, 4.4, 4.5, 4.8, 5.1 and 5.2 of the SmPC are updated. The Package Leaflet is updated in accordance. Version 5.0 of the RMP has also been agreed. In addition, the MAH took the opportunity to bring the PI in line with the QRD template version 10.4 and to implement corrections to the BG, CZ, DE, DK, EE, ES, FI, FR, HR, HU, IT, IS, LT, LV, MT, NL, NO, PL, PT, SE, SK, SV translations.

## Amendments to the marketing authorisation

In view of the data submitted with the variation, amendments to Annexes I and IIIB and to the Risk Management Plan are recommended.
